<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Knowledge</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Awareness of facts or being competent</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Knowledge_(disambiguation)" title="Knowledge (disambiguation)">Knowledge (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1025" data-file-width="1130" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg/330px-Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg/495px-Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg/660px-Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetradrachm_Athens_480-420BC_MBA_Lyon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Owl_of_Athena" title="Owl of Athena">owl of Athena</a> is a symbol of knowledge.</div></div></div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Epistemology_sidebar" title="Template:Epistemology sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Epistemology_sidebar" title="Template talk:Epistemology sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Epistemology_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Knowledge</b> can be defined as <a href="/wiki/Descriptive_knowledge" title="Descriptive knowledge">awareness of facts</a> or as <a href="/wiki/Procedural_knowledge" title="Procedural knowledge">practical skills</a>, and may also refer to <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_by_acquaintance" title="Knowledge by acquaintance">familiarity with objects or situations</a>. Knowledge of facts, also called <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositional</a> knowledge, is often defined as <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a> <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> that is distinct from opinion or guesswork by virtue of <a href="/wiki/Justification_(epistemology)" title="Justification (epistemology)">justification</a>. While there is wide agreement among philosophers that it is a form of true belief, many controversies in philosophy focus on justification: whether it is needed at all, how to understand it, and whether something else besides it is needed. These controversies intensified due to a series of <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem#Gettier's_two_original_counterexamples" title="Gettier problem">thought experiments by Edmund Gettier</a> and have provoked various alternative definitions. Some of them deny that justification is necessary and replace it, for example, with <a href="/wiki/Reliabilism" title="Reliabilism">reliability</a> or the manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Virtue_epistemology" title="Virtue epistemology">cognitive virtues</a>. Others contend that justification is needed but formulate additional requirements, for example, that no <a href="/wiki/Defeater" title="Defeater">defeaters</a> of the belief are present or that the person would not have the belief if it was false.
</p><p>Knowledge can be produced in many different ways. The most important source is <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a>, which refers to the usage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_senses" title="Five senses">five senses</a>. Many theorists also include <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a> as a source of knowledge, not of external physical objects, but of one's own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_states" title="Mental states">mental states</a>. Other sources often discussed include <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational_intuition" title="Rational intuition">rational intuition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Testimony" title="Testimony">testimony</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a>, some of these sources are basic in the sense that they can justify beliefs without depending on other mental states. This claim is rejected by <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentists</a>, who contend that a sufficient degree of coherence among all the mental states of the believer is necessary for knowledge.
</p><p>Many different aspects of knowledge are investigated and it plays a role in various disciplines. It is the primary subject of the field of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, which studies what we know, how we come to know it, and what it means to know something. The problem of the <i>value of knowledge</i> concerns the question of why knowledge is more valuable than mere true belief. <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Philosophical skepticism</a> is the controversial thesis that we lack any form of knowledge or that knowledge is impossible. <a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">Formal epistemology</a> studies, among other things, the rules governing how knowledge and related states behave and in what relations they stand to each other. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a> tries to acquire knowledge using the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, which is based on repeatable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimentation" title="Experimentation">experimentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurement</a>. Many religions hold that humans should seek knowledge and that God or the divine is the source of knowledge.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_knowledge" title="Definitions of knowledge">Definitions of knowledge</a></div>
<p>Numerous definitions of knowledge have been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The expressions "conception of knowledge", "theory of knowledge", and "analysis of knowledge" are sometimes utilized as synonyms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup> There is wide, though not universal, agreement among philosophers that knowledge can be characterized as a <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> success or an epistemic contact with <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a> and that propositional knowledge is a form of true belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> Most definitions of knowledge in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a> aim to determine the <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essential features</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositional</a> knowledge, which is also referred to as knowledge-that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> Knowledge-that can be expressed using that-clauses as in "I know that Dave is at home".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> It contrasts with knowledge-how (<a href="/wiki/Know-how" title="Know-how">know-how</a>) expressing practical <a href="/wiki/Competence_(human_resources)" title="Competence (human resources)">competence</a>, as in "she knows how to swim", and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_by_acquaintance" title="Knowledge by acquaintance">knowledge by acquaintance</a>, which refers to a familiarity with the known object based on previous direct <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knowing_How_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knowing_How-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many deep disagreements about knowledge's precise nature despite agreement on these general but vague characteristics. One definition that many philosophers consider to be standard is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a> (JTB). However, it has been criticized in diverse ways and many alternative definitions have been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> These disagreements have various sources that belong to the goals and methods within epistemology and other fields, or to differences concerning the standards of knowledge that people intend to uphold. Some theorists focus on knowledge's most salient features in their attempt to give a practically useful definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> Others try to provide a theoretically precise definition by listing the conditions that are <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">individually necessary and jointly sufficient</a>. The term "analysis of knowledge" is often used for this approach. It can be understood in analogy to how <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemists</a> analyze a sample by seeking a list of all the chemical elements composing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Others seek a common core among diverse examples of knowledge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> such as Paul Silva's "awareness first" epistemology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> or Barry Allen's definition of knowledge as "superlative artifactual performance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crumley_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crumley-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_methodology" title="Philosophical methodology">Methodological differences</a> concern whether researchers base their inquiry on abstract and general intuitions or hypotheses, or on concrete and specific cases, referred to as <i>methodism</i> and <i>particularism</i>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2013-3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2013-3-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Another source of disagreement is the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_language" title="Ordinary language">ordinary language</a> in one's inquiry: the weight given to how the term "knowledge" is used in everyday discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a>, for example, there is no clear-cut definition of knowledge since it is just a cluster of concepts related through <a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">family resemblance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different conceptions of the standards of knowledge are also responsible for various disagreements. Some epistemologists hold that knowledge demands very high requirements, like <a href="/wiki/Infallibility" title="Infallibility">infallibility</a>, and is therefore quite rare. Others see knowledge as a rather common phenomenon, prevalent in many everyday situations, without excessively high standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philpapers.org_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers.org-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justified_true_belief">Justified true belief</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belief#Justified_true_belief" title="Belief">Belief § Justified true belief</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_knowledge#Justified_true_belief" title="Definitions of knowledge">Definitions of knowledge § Justified true belief</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justified_true_belief.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="470" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Justified_true_belief.png/220px-Justified_true_belief.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Justified_true_belief.png/330px-Justified_true_belief.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Justified_true_belief.png/440px-Justified_true_belief.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justified_true_belief.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Knowledge is often defined as justified true belief.</div></div></div>
<p>Many philosophers define knowledge as <i>justified true belief</i> (JTB). This definition characterizes knowledge through three essential features: as (1) a <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> that is (2) <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a> and (3) <a href="/wiki/Justification_(epistemology)" title="Justification (epistemology)">justified</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> In the dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus</a></i> by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> pondered the distinction between knowledge and true belief but rejected the JTB definition of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parikh2017_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parikh2017-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordTheaetetus_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordTheaetetus-23">[23]</a></sup> The most widely accepted feature is truth: one can believe something false but one cannot know something false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> A few <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_language_philosophy" title="Ordinary language philosophy">ordinary language philosophers</a> have raised doubts that knowledge is a form of belief based on everyday expressions like "I do not believe that; I know it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> Most theorists reject this distinction and explain such expressions through ambiguities of <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main controversy surrounding the JTB definition concerns its third feature: justification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup> The motivation for including this component is that many true beliefs do apparently not amount to knowledge. Specifically, this covers cases of <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luck" title="Luck">lucky</a> guesses, or erroneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a>. The corresponding beliefs may even be true but it seems there is more to knowledge than just being right about something.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> The JTB definition solves this problem by identifying proper justification as the additional component needed, which is absent in the above-mentioned cases. Many philosophers have understood justification internalistically (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internalism" title="Internalism">internalism</a></i>): a belief is justified if it is supported by another <a href="/wiki/Mental_state" title="Mental state">mental state</a> of the person, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perceptual_experience" title="Perceptual experience">perceptual experience</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, or a second belief. This mental state has to constitute a sufficiently strong <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or reason for the believed proposition. Some modern versions modify the JTB definition by using an <i>externalist</i> conception of justification instead. This means that justification depends not just on factors internal to the subject but also on external factors. They can include, for example, that the belief was produced by a reliable process or that the believed fact caused the belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gettier_problem_and_alternatives">Gettier problem and alternatives</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problem</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg/300px-Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg/450px-Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg/600px-Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justified_True_Belief_model_of_knowledge.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Gettier problem is motivated by the idea that some justified true beliefs do not amount to knowledge.</div></div></div>
<p>The JTB definition came under severe criticism in the 20th century, when <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gettier" title="Edmund Gettier">Edmund Gettier</a> gave a series of counterexamples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPGettier_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPGettier-24">[24]</a></sup> They purport to present concrete cases of justified true beliefs that fail to constitute knowledge. The reason for their failure is usually a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemic_luck" title="Epistemic luck">epistemic luck</a>: the justification is not relevant to the truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> In a well-known example, there is a country road with many <a href="/wiki/Potemkin_village" title="Potemkin village">barn facades</a> and only one real barn. The person driving is not aware of this, stops by a lucky coincidence in front of the real barn, and forms the belief that he is in front of a barn. It has been argued that this justified true belief does not constitute knowledge since the person wouldn't have been able to tell the difference without the fortuitous accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDefeaters2b_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDefeaters2b-27">[27]</a></sup> So even though the belief is justified, it is a lucky coincidence that it is also true. The responses to these counterexamples have been diverse. According to some, they show that the JTB definition of knowledge is deeply flawed and that a radical reconceptualization of knowledge is necessary, often by denying justification a role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup> This can happen, for example, by replacing justification with reliability or by understanding knowledge as the manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_virtue" title="Intellectual virtue">cognitive virtues</a>. Other approaches include defining it in regard to the cognitive role it plays in providing reasons for doing or thinking something or seeing it as the most general factive mental state operator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> Various theorists are diametrically opposed to the radical reconceptualization and either deny that Gettier cases pose problems or they try to solve them by making smaller modifications to how justification is defined. Such approaches result in a minimal modification of the JTB definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between these two extremes, some philosophers have suggested various moderate departures. They agree that the JTB definition is a step in the right direction: justified true belief is a necessary condition of knowledge. However, they disagree that it is a sufficient condition. They hold instead that an additional criterion, some feature <i>X</i>, is necessary for knowledge. For this reason, they are often referred to as <i>JTB+X</i> definitions of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durán2018_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durán2018-28">[28]</a></sup> A closely related approach speaks not of justification but of warrant and defines warrant as justification together with whatever else is necessary to arrive at knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comesaña2005_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comesaña2005-29">[29]</a></sup> Many candidates for the fourth feature have been suggested. In this regard, knowledge may be defined as justified true belief that does not depend on any false beliefs, that there are no <a href="/wiki/Defeater" title="Defeater">defeaters</a> present, or that the person would not have the belief if it was false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDefeaters2b_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDefeaters2b-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Simon_Blackburn" title="Simon Blackburn">Simon Blackburn</a>, those who have a justified true belief 'through a defect, flaw, or failure' fail to have knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkham1984_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkham1984-30">[30]</a></sup> Such and similar definitions are successful at avoiding many of the original Gettier cases. However, they often fall prey to newly conceived counterexamples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> To avoid all possible cases, it may be necessary to find a criterion that excludes all forms of epistemic luck. It has been argued that such a criterion would set the required standards of knowledge very high: the belief has to be infallible to succeed in all cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-9"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kraft2012_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kraft2012-31">[31]</a></sup> This would mean that very few of our beliefs amount to knowledge, if any.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-10"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philpapers.org_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers.org-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Kirkham" title="Richard Kirkham">Richard Kirkham</a> suggests that our definition of knowledge requires that the evidence for the belief necessitates its truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkham1984_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkham1984-30">[30]</a></sup> There is still very little consensus in the academic discourse as to which of the proposed modifications or reconceptualizations is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<p>The English word <i>knowledge</i> can translate a variety of words in other languages that refer to different states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> The Latin words <i>cognitio</i> and <i>scientia</i> can both be translated as "knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> Romance languages have two major verbs that would both be translated as "to know": for example, <i>connaître</i> and <i>savoir</i> in French or <i>conocer</i> and <i>saber</i> in Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> In ancient Greek, there were four such important knowledge words: <i><a href="/wiki/Episteme" title="Episteme">epistēmē</a></i> (unchanging theoretical knowledge), <i><a href="/wiki/Techne" title="Techne">technē</a></i> (expert technical knowledge), <i><a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">mētis</a></i> (strategic knowledge), and <i><a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnōsis</a></i> (personal intellectual knowledge).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-15">[15]</a></sup> All these different types of knowledge can be considered forms of cognitive success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propositional_knowledge">Propositional knowledge</span></h3>
<p>Propositional knowledge, also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Descriptive_knowledge" title="Descriptive knowledge">descriptive knowledge</a>, is the paradigmatic type of knowledge in analytic philosophy, and various classifications are used to distinguish between its different subtypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-10"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-11"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> The distinctions between the major types are usually drawn based on the linguistic formulations used to express them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAnalysis_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAnalysis-1">[1]</a></sup> Propositional knowledge is <i>propositional</i> in the sense that it involves a relation to a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>. Since propositions are often expressed through that-clauses, it is also referred to as <i>knowledge-that</i>, as in "Akari knows that <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a> is the capital of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-12"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> In this case, Akari stands in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relation_(philosophy)" title="Relation (philosophy)">relation</a> of knowing to the proposition "Canberra is the capital of Australia". Closely related types of knowledge are know-wh, for example, knowing where the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a> is or knowing who killed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._F._Kennedy" title="J. F. Kennedy">J. F. Kennedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-13"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup> These expressions are normally understood as types of propositional knowledge since they usually can be paraphrased using a that-clause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-14"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2013-1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2013-1-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important distinction among propositional forms of knowledge is between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apriori" title="Apriori">apriori</a> and aposteriori knowledge. For aposteriori knowledge, its justification is based on <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a>, like sensory experience. It contrasts with apriori knowledge, which is based on pure reason or rational intuition without the need for sensation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> Apriori knowledge is sometimes identified with <i>innate knowledge</i>, which is inborn and does not need to be newly learned. Popular suggestions for this type include the knowledge of basic mathematical claims, like "2 + 2 = 4". It is distinguished from acquired knowledge, which the person needs to learn first in order to possess it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> Two closely related distinctions are those between necessary and <a href="/wiki/Contingency_(philosophy)" title="Contingency (philosophy)">contingent</a> knowledge, based on whether it is possible at all that the known proposition is false, as well as between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytic_proposition" title="Analytic proposition">analytic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analytic%E2%80%93synthetic_distinction" title="Analytic–synthetic distinction">synthetic</a> knowledge, based on whether the truth of the known proposition depends only on the meaning of the terms it uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>A different distinction is that between occurrent and dispositional knowledge. It mirrors the distinction between <a href="/wiki/Belief#Occurrent_and_dispositional" title="Belief">occurrent and dispositional beliefs</a>: to know occurrently means to entertain the corresponding representation currently, to be aware of it. "Dispositional knowledge" refers to the mere ability to do so without its execution. In this regard, a person fully immersed in a go-kart race has dispositional but not occurrent knowledge of where their home is. The reason is that they are currently occupied with something else but could easily provide this information if they stopped and focused on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-propositional_knowledge">Non-propositional knowledge</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bicycle_ride.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1641" data-file-width="1641" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bicycle_ride.jpg/220px-Bicycle_ride.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bicycle_ride.jpg/330px-Bicycle_ride.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bicycle_ride.jpg/440px-Bicycle_ride.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bicycle_ride.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Knowing how to ride a bicycle is one form of non-propositional knowledge.</div></div></div>
<p>For non-propositional knowledge, no essential relation to a proposition is involved. The two most well-known forms are knowledge-how (<a href="/wiki/Know-how" title="Know-how">know-how</a> or <a href="/wiki/Procedural_knowledge" title="Procedural knowledge">procedural knowledge</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_by_acquaintance" title="Knowledge by acquaintance">knowledge by acquaintance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knowing_How_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Knowing_How-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-15"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> The term "know-how" refers to some form of practical ability or skill. It can be defined as having the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Competence_(human_resources)" title="Competence (human resources)">competence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-16"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2013-1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2013-1-32">[32]</a></sup> Examples include knowing how to ride a bicycle or knowing how to play the guitar. Some of the abilities responsible for know-how may also involve certain forms of knowledge-that, as in knowing how to prove a <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematical</a> theorem. But this is not generally the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> It is usually argued that mainly humans and maybe other higher animals possess propositional knowledge since it requires an advanced form of mind. Practical knowledge, on the other hand, is more common in the animal kingdom. In this regard, an <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a> knows how to walk even though it presumably lacks a mind sufficiently developed enough to stand in a relation to the corresponding proposition by representing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2013-1_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2013-1-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Knowledge by acquaintance refers to familiarity with an individual that results from direct experiential contact with this individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-17"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-11"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> It often, but not exclusively, concerns a relation to a person. On the linguistic level, it does not require a that-clause and can be expressed using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_object" title="Direct object">direct object</a>. So when someone claims that they know Wladimir Klitschko personally, they are expressing that they had a certain kind of contact with him and not that they know a certain fact about him. This is usually understood to mean that it constitutes a relation to a concrete individual and not to a proposition. Knowledge by acquaintance plays a central role in <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>'s epistemology. He contrasts it with knowledge by description, which is a form of propositional knowledge not based on direct perceptual experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> So by watching a documentary about <a href="/wiki/Wladimir_Klitschko" title="Wladimir Klitschko">Wladimir Klitschko</a>, the viewer may acquire various forms of knowledge by description about him, for example, about his nationality or his career in boxing, without acquiring knowledge by acquaintance of him. However, there is some controversy about whether it is possible to acquire knowledge by acquaintance in its pure non-propositional form. In this regard, some theorists have suggested that it might be better to understand it as one type of propositional knowledge that is only expressed in a grammatically different way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-11"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_distinctions">Other distinctions</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="A_priori_and_a_posteriori">A priori and a posteriori</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">A priori and a posteriori</a></div>
<p>The distinction between a priori and a posteriori knowledge came to prominence in <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>'s philosophy and is often discussed in the academic literature. To which category a knowledge attitude belongs depends on the role of <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> in its formation and justification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2020_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2020-40">[40]</a></sup> To know something a posteriori means to know it on the basis of experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAPosteriori_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAPosteriori-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moser2022_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moser2022-42">[42]</a></sup> For example, to know that it is currently raining or that the baby is crying belongs to a posteriori knowledge since it is based on some form of experience, like visual or auditory experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup> A priori knowledge, on the other hand, is possible without any experience to justify or support the known proposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2020_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2020-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAPriori_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAPriori-43">[43]</a></sup> Mathematical knowledge, for example, that 2 + 2 = 4, is a paradigmatic case of a priori knowledge since no empirical investigation is necessary to confirm this fact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAPriori_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAPriori-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moser2022_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moser2022-42">[42]</a></sup> The distinction between a posteriori and a priori knowledge is usually equated with the distinction between empirical and non-empirical knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moser2022_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moser2022-42">[42]</a></sup> This distinction pertains primarily to knowledge but it can also be applied to <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a> or arguments. For example, an a priori proposition is a proposition that can be known independently of experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The prime example of the relevant experience in question is <a href="/wiki/Experience#Perception" title="Experience">sensory experience</a>. However, some non-sensory experiences, like memory and introspection, are often included as well. But certain conscious phenomena are excluded in this context. For example, the conscious phenomenon of a rational insight into the solution of a mathematical problem does not make the resulting knowledge a posteriori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2020_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2020-40">[40]</a></sup> It is sometimes pointed out that, in a trivial sense, some form of experience is required even for a priori knowledge: the experience needed to learn the language in which the claim is expressed. For a priori knowledge, this is the only form of experience required. For this reason, knowing that "all bachelors are unmarried" is considered a form of a priori knowledge since, given an understanding of the terms "bachelor" and "unmarried", no further experience is necessary to know that it is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2020_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2020-40">[40]</a></sup> One difficulty for a priori knowledge is to explain how it is possible. It is usually seen as unproblematic that one can come to know things through experience but it is not clear how knowledge is possible without experience. One of the earliest solutions to this problem is due to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, who argues that, in the context of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>, the soul already possesses the knowledge and just needs to recollect or remember it to access it again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAPriori_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAPriori-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolf_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woolf-44">[44]</a></sup> A similar explanation is given by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, who holds that a priori knowledge exists as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_knowledge" title="Innate knowledge">innate knowledge</a> present in the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> of each human.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAPriori_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAPriori-43">[43]</a></sup> A different approach is to posit a special mental faculty responsible for this type of knowledge, often referred to as <i>rational insight</i> or <i>rational intuition</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinction between a priori and a posteriori knowledge is closely related to two other distinctions: the distinction between <a href="/wiki/Analytic%E2%80%93synthetic_distinction" title="Analytic–synthetic distinction">analytic and synthetic propositions</a> and the distinction between necessary and <a href="/wiki/Contingency_(philosophy)" title="Contingency (philosophy)">contingent</a> propositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-6"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casullo2006_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casullo2006-45">[45]</a></sup> Traditionally, it was often assumed that these distinctions coincide. On this view, a priori knowledge concerns propositions that are analytic and necessary while a posteriori knowledge is about propositions that are synthetic and contingent. However, this position is rejected by many contemporary philosophers. One reason is that these distinctions belong to different fields. The a priori–a posteriori distinction belongs to <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and is about how one knows things. The analytic–synthetic distinction pertains to <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a> and concerns how the meanings of terms make a proposition true. The necessary–contingent distinction is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical" title="Metaphysical">metaphysical</a> and asks, for example, whether a proposition is true in all <a href="/wiki/Possible_world" title="Possible world">possible worlds</a> or just in some of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-7"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup> Various discussions in the academic literature concern the question of how these distinctions overlap or fail to overlap, for example, on cases of synthetic a priori truths or of contingent a priori truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baehr2022_39-8"><a href="#cite_note-Baehr2022-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casullo2006_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Casullo2006-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-knowledge">Self-knowledge</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Self-knowledge_(psychology)" title="Self-knowledge (psychology)">Self-knowledge (psychology)</a></div>
<p>"Self-knowledge" usually refers to a person's knowledge of their own sensations, thoughts, beliefs, and other mental states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Self_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Self-47">[47]</a></sup> A number of questions regarding self-knowledge have been the subject of extensive debates in philosophy, including whether self-knowledge differs from other types of knowledge, whether we have privileged self-knowledge compared to knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_other_minds" title="Problem of other minds">other minds</a>, and the nature of our acquaintance with ourselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Self_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Self-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> expressed skepticism about whether we could ever have self-knowledge over and above our immediate awareness of a "bundle of perceptions", which was part of his broader skepticism about <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">personal identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Self_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Self-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Situated_knowledge">Situated knowledge</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Situated knowledges" redirects here. For the Donna Haraway essay, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Situated_Knowledges" title="Situated Knowledges">Situated Knowledges</a>.</div>
<p>Situated knowledge is knowledge specific to a particular situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APASituatedKnowledge_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-APASituatedKnowledge-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter2009_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2009-49">[49]</a></sup> It is closely related to practical or tacit knowledge, which is learned and applied in specific circumstances. This especially concerns certain forms of acquiring knowledge, such as <a href="/wiki/Trial_and_error" title="Trial and error">trial and error</a> or learning from <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harawy1988_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harawy1988-50">[50]</a></sup> In this regard, situated knowledge usually lacks a more explicit structure and is not articulated in terms of universal ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter2009_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2009-49">[49]</a></sup> The term is often used in <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernism</a> to point out that many forms of knowledge are not absolute but depend on the concrete historical, cultural, and linguistic context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APASituatedKnowledge_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-APASituatedKnowledge-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter2009_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2009-49">[49]</a></sup> Understood in this way, it is frequently utilized to argue against absolute or universal knowledge claims stated in the scientific discourse. <a href="/wiki/Donna_Haraway" title="Donna Haraway">Donna Haraway</a> is a prominent defender of this position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harawy1988_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harawy1988-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson2001_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2001-51">[51]</a></sup> One of her arguments is based on the idea that perception is embodied and is not a universal "gaze from nowhere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harawy1988_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harawy1988-50">[50]</a></sup> Some interpreters associate with this position a form of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology#Epistemic_relativism" title="Epistemology">epistemological relativism</a> because of how knowledge depends on the local conditions of the culture in which it arises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APASituatedKnowledge_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-APASituatedKnowledge-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_and_lower_knowledge">Higher and lower knowledge</span></h4>
<p>Many forms of eastern spirituality and religion distinguish between <i>higher</i> and <i>lower knowledge</i>. They are also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Para_Vidya" title="Para Vidya">para vidya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vidya_(philosophy)#Hierarchy_of_knowledge" title="Vidya (philosophy)">apara vidya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Two_truths_doctrine" title="Two truths doctrine">two truths doctrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. Lower knowledge is based on the senses and the intellect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordTwoTruths_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordTwoTruths-53">[53]</a></sup> In this regard, all forms of empirical and objective knowledge belong to this category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HinduVidya_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-HinduVidya-54">[54]</a></sup> Most of the knowledge needed in one's everyday functioning is lower knowledge. It is about mundane or conventional things that are in tune with common sense, like that mice are smaller than elephants. It is relevant to many practical issues, like how to repair a car or how to persuade a customer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordTwoTruths_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordTwoTruths-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mishra_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mishra-55">[55]</a></sup> Scientific knowledge, for example, that the chemical composition of water is H<sub>2</sub>O, is often seen as one of the most advanced forms of lower knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aurobindo_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aurobindo-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Higher knowledge, on the other hand, is understood as knowledge of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_absolute" title="The absolute">the absolute</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_self" title="True self">true self</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_reality" title="Ultimate reality">ultimate reality</a>. It belongs neither to the external world of physical objects nor to the internal world of the experience of emotions and concepts. Many spiritual teachings emphasize the increased importance, or sometimes even exclusive importance, of higher knowledge in comparison to lower knowledge. This is usually based on the idea that achieving higher knowledge is one of the central steps on the spiritual path. In this regard, higher knowledge is seen as what frees the individual from ignorance, helps them realize God, or liberates them from the cycle of rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HinduVidya_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-HinduVidya-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mishra_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mishra-55">[55]</a></sup> This is often combined with the view that lower knowledge is in some way based on a delusion: it belongs to the realm of mere appearances or <a href="/wiki/Maya_(religion)" title="Maya (religion)">Maya</a>, while higher knowledge manages to view the reality underlying these appearances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aurobindo_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aurobindo-56">[56]</a></sup> In the Buddhist tradition, the attainment of higher knowledge or ultimate truth is often associated with seeing the world from the perspective of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunyata" title="Sunyata">sunyata</a>, i.e. as a form of <a href="/wiki/Emptiness" title="Emptiness">emptiness</a> lacking inherent existence or <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">intrinsic nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordTwoTruths_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordTwoTruths-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_knowledge">Sources of knowledge</span></h2>
<p>Sources of knowledge are ways how people come to know things or how knowledge is created. Different sources of knowledge are discussed in the academic literature, often in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mental faculties</a> responsible. They include perception, introspection, memory, inference, and testimony. However, not everyone agrees that all of them actually lead to knowledge. Usually, <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> or observation, i.e. using one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_senses" title="Five senses">five senses</a>, is identified as the most important source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-18"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-12"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> So knowing that the baby is sleeping constitutes observational knowledge if it was caused by a perception of the snoring baby. But this would not be the case if one learned about this fact through a telephone conversation with one's spouse. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_realism" title="Direct realism">Direct realists</a> explain observational knowledge by holding that perception constitutes a direct contact with the perceived object. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indirect_realism" title="Indirect realism">Indirect realists</a>, on the other hand, contend that this contact happens indirectly: we can only directly perceive <a href="/wiki/Sense_data" title="Sense data">sense data</a>, which are then interpreted as representing external objects. This distinction is important since it affects whether the knowledge of external objects is direct or indirect and may thus have an impact on how certain the knowledge is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">Introspection</a> is often seen in analogy to perception as a source of knowledge, not of external physical objects, but of internal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_states" title="Mental states">mental states</a>. Traditionally, various theorists have ascribed a special epistemic status to introspection by claiming that it is infallible or that there is no introspective difference between appearance and reality. However, this claim has been contested in the contemporary discourse. Critics argue that it may be possible, for example, to mistake an unpleasant itch for a pain or to confuse the experience of a slight ellipse for the experience of a circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> Perceptual and introspective knowledge often act as a form of fundamental or basic knowledge. According to some <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricists</a>, perceptual knowledge is the only source of basic knowledge and provides the foundation for all other knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-19"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-13"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">Memory</a> is usually identified as another source of knowledge. It differs from perception and introspection in that it is not as independent or fundamental as they are since it depends on other previous experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi2002_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Audi2002-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-14"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> The faculty of memory retains knowledge acquired in the past and makes it accessible in the present, as when remembering a past event or a friend's phone number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It is generally considered a reliable source of knowledge, but it may deceive us at times nonetheless, either because the original experience was unreliable or because the memory degraded and does not accurately represent the original experience anymore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-15"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Knowledge based on perception, introspection, or memory may also give rise to <i>inferential knowledge</i>, which comes about when reasoning is applied to draw inferences from another known fact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-20"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-14"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-16"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> In this regard, the perceptual knowledge of a Czech stamp on a postcard may give rise to the inferential knowledge that one's friend is visiting the Czech Republic. According to <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalists</a>, some forms of knowledge are completely independent of observation and introspection. They are needed to explain how certain apriori beliefs, like the mathematical belief that 2 + 2 = 4, constitute knowledge. Some theorists hold that the faculty of <i>pure reason</i> or <i>rational intuition</i> is responsible in these cases since there seem to be no sensory perceptions that could justify such general and abstract knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi2002_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Audi2002-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> However, difficulties in providing a clear account of pure reason or rational intuition have led various <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirically minded</a> epistemologists to doubt that they constitute independent sources of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-21"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-15"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-17"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> A closely related approach is to hold that this type of knowledge is <i>innate</i>. According to Plato's theory of recollection, for example, it is accessed through a special form of remembering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-22"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-16"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Testimony" title="Testimony">Testimony</a> is often included as an additional source of knowledge. Unlike the other sources, it is not tied to one specific cognitive faculty. Instead, it is based on the idea that one person can come to know a fact because another person talks about this fact. Testimony can happen in numerous ways, like regular speech, a letter, the newspaper, or an online blog. The problem of testimony consists in clarifying under what circumstances and why it constitutes a source of knowledge. A popular response is that it depends on the reliability of the person pronouncing the testimony: only testimony from reliable sources can lead to knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-18"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_of_knowledge">Structure of knowledge</span></h2>
<p>The expression "structure of knowledge" refers to the way in which the mental states of a person need to be related to each other for knowledge to arise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Most theorists hold that, among other things, an agent has to have good reasons for holding a belief if this belief is to amount to knowledge. So when challenged, the agent may justify their belief by referring to their reason for holding it. In many cases, this reason is itself a belief that may as well be challenged. So when the agent believes that Ford cars are cheaper than BMWs because they believe to have heard this from a reliable source, they may be challenged to justify why they believe that their source is reliable. If it turns out that their reasons are not well supported, this also affects the epistemic status of the original belief. However, whatever support they present may also be challenged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-12"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-19"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> This threatens to lead to an <a href="/wiki/Infinite_regress" title="Infinite regress">infinite regress</a> since the epistemic status at each step depends on the epistemic status of the previous step.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Theories of the structure of knowledge offer responses for how to solve this problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-13"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-20"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foundationalism,_coherentism,_infinitism.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg/400px-Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg/600px-Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg/800px-Foundationalism%2C_coherentism%2C_infinitism.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foundationalism,_coherentism,_infinitism.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Foundationalism, coherentism, and infinitism are theories of the structure of knowledge. The black arrows symbolize how one belief supports another belief.</div></div></div>
<p>The three most common theories are <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infinitism" title="Infinitism">infinitism</a>. Foundationalists and coherentists deny the existence of this infinite regress, in contrast to infinitists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-14"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-21"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> According to foundationalists, some <i>basic reasons</i> have their epistemic status independent of other reasons and thereby constitute the endpoint of the regress. Against this view, it has been argued that the concept of "basic reason" is contradictory: there should be a reason for why some reasons are basic and others are non-basic, in which case the basic reasons would depend on another reason after all and would therefore not be basic. An additional problem consists in finding plausible candidates for basic reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-15"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-22"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coherentists and infinitists avoid these problems by denying the distinction between basic and non-basic reasons. Coherentists argue that there is only a finite number of reasons, which mutually support each other and thereby ensure each other's epistemic status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-16"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-23"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> Their critics contend that this constitutes the fallacy of <a href="/wiki/Circular_reasoning" title="Circular reasoning">circular reasoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> For example, if belief <i>b1</i> supports belief <i>b2</i> and belief <i>b2</i> supports belief <i>b1</i>, the agent has a reason for accepting one belief if they already have the other. However, their mutual support alone is not a good reason for newly accepting both beliefs at once. A closely related issue is that there can be various distinct sets of coherent beliefs and coherentists face the problem of explaining why we should accept one coherent set rather than another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-17"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-24"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> For infinitists, in contrast to foundationalists and coherentists, there is an infinite number of reasons. This position faces the problem of explaining how human knowledge is possible at all since it seems that the human mind is limited and cannot possess an infinite amount of reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-18"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup> In their traditional forms, foundationalists, coherentists and infinitists all face the <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problem</a>, i.e. that having a reason or justification for a true belief is not sufficient for knowledge in cases where cognitive luck is responsible for the success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-19"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Value_of_knowledge">Value of knowledge</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg/220px-Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg/330px-Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg/440px-Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Los_portadores_de_la_antorcha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Los portadores de la antorcha (The Torch-Bearers)</i> – Sculpture by <a href="/wiki/Anna_Hyatt_Huntington" title="Anna Hyatt Huntington">Anna Hyatt Huntington</a> symbolizing the transmission of knowledge from one generation to the next (<a href="/wiki/Complutense_University_of_Madrid" title="Complutense University of Madrid">Ciudad Universitaria, Madrid, Spain</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The value of knowledge is an important topic in epistemology. Its main question is whether or why knowledge is more valuable than mere true belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson2011_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson2011-72">[72]</a></sup> There is wide agreement that knowledge is good in some sense. For example, knowledge can help a student pass the exam or ensure that a doctor prescribes the right medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson2011_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson2011-72">[72]</a></sup> However, the thesis that knowledge is better than true belief is more controversial. An early discussion of this problem is found in Plato's <a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a> in relation to the claim that both knowledge and true belief can successfully guide <a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action (philosophy)">action</a> and, therefore, have apparently the same value. For example, it seems that mere true belief is as effective as knowledge when trying to find the way to <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> According to Plato, knowledge is better because it is more stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson2011_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson2011-72">[72]</a></sup> A different approach is to hold that knowledge gets its additional value from justification. However, if the value in question is understood primarily as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instrumental_value" title="Instrumental value">instrumental value</a>, it is not clear in what sense knowledge is better than mere true belief since they are usually equally useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The problem of the value of knowledge is often discussed in relation to <a href="/wiki/Reliabilism" title="Reliabilism">reliabilism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virtue_epistemology" title="Virtue epistemology">virtue epistemology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson2011_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson2011-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard-APQ_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard-APQ-74">[74]</a></sup> Reliabilism can be defined as the thesis that knowledge is reliably-formed true belief. On this view, it seems difficult to explain how a reliable belief-forming process adds additional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup> According to an analogy by <a href="/wiki/Linda_Zagzebski" title="Linda Zagzebski">Linda Zagzebski</a>, a cup of coffee made by a reliable coffee machine has the same value as an equally good cup of coffee made by an unreliable coffee machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turri2021_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turri2021-75">[75]</a></sup> This difficulty in solving the value problem is sometimes used as an argument against reliabilism. Virtue epistemologists have a different approach to the value problem.
