They see knowledge as the manifestation of cognitive virtues and can thus argue that knowledge has additional value due to its association with virtue. However, not everyone agrees that knowledge actually has additional value over true belief. A similar view is defended by <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Kvanvig" title="Jonathan Kvanvig">Jonathan Kvanvig</a>, who argues that the main epistemic value resides not in knowledge but in <a href="/wiki/Understanding" title="Understanding">understanding</a>, which implies grasping how one's beliefs cohere with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard2022_71-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2022-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson2011_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson2011-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_skepticism">Philosophical skepticism</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Philosophical skepticism</a> in its strongest form, also referred to as <i>global skepticism</i>, is the thesis that we lack any form of knowledge or that knowledge is impossible. This position is quite radical and very few philosophers have explicitly defended it. However, it has been influential nonetheless, usually in a negative sense: many researchers see it as a serious challenge to any epistemological theory and often try to show how their preferred theory overcomes it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-20"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-23"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-25"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> For example, it is commonly accepted that <a href="/wiki/Experience#Perception" title="Experience">perceptual experience</a> constitutes a source of knowledge. However, according to the <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">dream argument</a>, this is not the case since dreaming provides unreliable information and since the agent could be dreaming right now. In this case, they would be unable to distinguish actual perceptual experience from the dreaming experience. Since they may be dreaming at any time without being aware of this, it is then argued that there is no perceptual knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-21"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-24"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-17"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> A similar often cited <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> assumes that the agent is actually a <a href="/wiki/Brain_in_a_vat" title="Brain in a vat">brain in a vat</a> that is just fed electrical stimuli. Such a brain would have the false impression of having a body and interacting with the external world. The basic thrust of the argument is the same: since the agent is unable to tell the difference, they do not know that they have a body responsible for reliable perceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-26"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>One issue revealed through these thought experiments is the problem of <a href="/wiki/Underdetermination" title="Underdetermination">underdetermination</a>: that the evidence available is not sufficient to make a rational decision between competing theories. And if two contrary hypotheses explain the appearances equally well then the agent is not justified in believing one of those hypotheses rather than the other. Based on this premise, the general skeptic just has to argue that this is true for all our knowledge, that there is always an alternative and very different explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-27"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> Another skeptic argument is based on the idea that human cognition is fallible and therefore lacks absolute certainty. More specific arguments target particular theories of knowledge, such as foundationalism or coherentism, and try to show that their concept of knowledge is deeply flawed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-22"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-28"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> An important argument against global skepticism is that it seems to contradict itself: the claim that there is no knowledge appears to constitute a knowledge-claim itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-18"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup> Other responses come from common sense philosophy and reject global skepticism based on the fact that it contradicts common sense. It is then argued against skepticism by seeing common sense as more reliable than the abstract reasoning cited in favor of skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-29"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain less radical forms of skepticism deny that knowledge exists within a specific area or discipline, sometimes referred to as <i>local</i> or <i>selective skepticism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-25"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-19"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-30"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> It is often motivated by the idea that certain phenomena do not accurately represent their subject matter. They may thus lead to false impressions concerning its nature. External world skeptics hold that we can only know about our own sensory impressions and experiences but not about the external world. This is based on the idea that beliefs about the external world are mediated through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_senses" title="The senses">the senses</a>. The senses are faulty at times and may thus show things that are not really there. This problem is avoided on the level of sensory impressions, which are given to the experiencer directly without an intermediary. In this sense, the person may be wrong about seeing a red Ferrari in the street (it might have been a Maserati or a mere light reflection) but they cannot be wrong about having a sensory impression of seeing a patch of red color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-26"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-20"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-31"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> The inverse path is taken by some materialists, who accept the existence of the external physical world but deny the existence of the internal realm of mind and consciousness based on the difficulty of explaining how the two realms can exist together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehrer2015_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lehrer2015-5">[5]</a></sup> Other forms of local skepticism accept scientific knowledge but deny the possibility of moral knowledge, for example, because there is no reliable way to empirically measure whether a moral claim is true or false.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPKnowledge_9-27"><a href="#cite_note-IEPKnowledge-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The issue of the definition and standards of knowledge is central to the question of whether skepticism in its different forms is true. If very high standards are used, for example, that knowledge implies infallibility, then skepticism becomes more plausible. In this case, the skeptic only has to show that no belief is absolutely certain, that while the actual belief is true, it could have been false. However, the more these standards are weakened to how the term is used in everyday language, the less plausible skepticism becomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaEpistemology_10-21"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaEpistemology-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagzebski1999_7-15"><a href="#cite_note-Zagzebski1999-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-32"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_various_disciplines">In various disciplines</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_epistemology">Formal epistemology</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">Formal epistemology</a> studies knowledge using formal tools, such as mathematics and logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> An important issue in this field concerns the epistemic principles of knowledge. They are rules governing how knowledge and related states behave and in what relations they stand to each other. The transparency principle, also referred to as the <i>luminosity of knowledge</i>, is an often discussed principle. It states that knowing something implies the second-order knowledge that one knows it. So if Heike knows that today is Monday, then she also knows that she knows that today is Monday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEpistemology_6-33"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEpistemology-6">[6]</a></sup> According to the <i>conjunction principle</i>, having two justified beliefs in two separate propositions implies that the agent is also justified in believing in the conjunction of these two propositions. The <i>closure principle</i> states that if the agent has a justified belief in one proposition and this proposition entails another proposition, then the agent is also justified in believing this other proposition. The <i>evidence transfer principle</i> applies this idea to evidence: if, in the case above, a certain piece of evidence justifies the first belief then it also justifies the second belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeKnowledge_8-23"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeKnowledge-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">Philosophy of science</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_(philosopher,_statesman,_born_1561).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1337" data-file-width="814" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3d/Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg/170px-Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3d/Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg/255px-Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3d/Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg/340px-Francis_Bacon_%28philosopher%2C_statesman%2C_born_1561%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francis_Bacon_(philosopher,_statesman,_born_1561).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Francis_Bacon" title="Sir Francis Bacon">Sir Francis Bacon</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Scientia_potentia_est" title="Scientia potentia est">Knowledge is Power</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> has made a significant contribution to how knowledge of the physical world and its phenomena is acquired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> To be termed scientific, a method of <a href="/wiki/Inquiry" title="Inquiry">inquiry</a> must be based on gathering <a href="/wiki/Observable" title="Observable">observable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurable</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> subject to specific principles of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> and experimentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The scientific method consists of the collection of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> through <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experimentation</a>, and the formulation and testing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Science, and the nature of scientific knowledge have also become the subject of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy</a>. As science itself has developed, scientific knowledge now includes a broader usage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_science" title="Hard and soft science">soft sciences</a> such as biology and the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Francis_Bacon" title="Sir Francis Bacon">Sir Francis Bacon</a> was critical of the historical development of the scientific method; his works established and popularized an inductive methodology for scientific inquiry. His aphorism, "<a href="/wiki/Scientia_potentia_est" title="Scientia potentia est">knowledge is power</a>", is found in the Meditations Sacrae (1597).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<p>Knowledge plays a central role in many <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a>. Knowledge claims about the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_doctrine" title="Religious doctrine">religious doctrines</a> about how each one should live their lives are found in almost every culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2022_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2022-85">[85]</a></sup> However, such knowledge claims are often controversial and are commonly rejected by <a href="/wiki/Religious_skepticism" title="Religious skepticism">religious skeptics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheists" title="Atheists">atheists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penelhum1971_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penelhum1971-86">[86]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Religious_epistemology" title="Religious epistemology">epistemology of religion</a> is the field of inquiry that investigates whether <a href="/wiki/Belief_in_God" title="Belief in God">belief in God</a> and in other religious doctrines is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational" title="Rational">rational</a> and amounts to knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forrest2021_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest2021-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2022_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2022-85">[85]</a></sup> It is different from other forms of epistemology because of its unique subject matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penelhum1971_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Penelhum1971-86">[86]</a></sup> One important view in this field is <a href="/wiki/Evidentialism" title="Evidentialism">evidentialism</a>. It states that belief in religious doctrines is justified if it is supported by sufficient <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>. Suggested examples of evidence for religious doctrines include <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">religious experiences</a> such as direct contact with the divine or inner testimony as when hearing God's voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forrest2021_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest2021-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2022_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2022-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty2014_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dougherty2014-88">[88]</a></sup> However, evidentialists often reject that belief in religious doctrines amount to knowledge based on the claim that we lack sufficient evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2022_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2022-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forrest2021_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest2021-87">[87]</a></sup> A famous saying in this regard is due to <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>. When asked how he would justify his lack of belief in God when facing His judgment after death, he replied "Not enough evidence, God! Not enough evidence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2022_85-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2022-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, religious teachings about the existence and nature of God are not always understood as knowledge claims by their defenders and some explicitly state that the proper attitude towards such doctrines is not knowledge but <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>. This is often combined with the assumption that these doctrines are true but cannot be fully understood by reason or verified through rational inquiry. For this reason, it is claimed that one should accept them even though they do not amount to knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penelhum1971_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Penelhum1971-86">[86]</a></sup> Such a view is reflected in a famous saying by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> where he claims that he "had to deny knowledge in order to make room for faith."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Distinct religions often differ from each other concerning the doctrines they proclaim as well as their understanding of the role of knowledge in religious practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouquet1962_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bouquet1962-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h4>
<p>In many expressions of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> knowledge is one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_gifts_of_the_Holy_Spirit" title="Seven gifts of the Holy Spirit">seven gifts of the Holy Spirit</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> points out, "the knowledge that comes from the Holy Spirit, however, is not limited to human knowledge; it is a special gift, which leads us to grasp, through creation, the greatness and love of God and his profound relationship with every creature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</span></h4>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnostic" title="Gnostic">Gnostic</a> beliefs, everyone is said to possess a piece of the highest good or <a href="/wiki/Monad_(Gnosticism)" title="Monad (Gnosticism)">Ultimate God</a> deep within themselves that had fallen from the spiritual world into the bodies of humans, sometimes called a <a href="/wiki/Divine_spark" title="Divine spark">divine spark</a>. It is trapped in their material bodies created by the inferior God or <a href="/wiki/Demiurge#Gnosticism" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a> unless secret knowledge from the outside universe called <a href="/wiki/Gnosis#Gnosticism" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a> is achieved. The one who brings such knowledge is considered the savior or redeemer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> Scriptures present two kinds of knowledge, <i>Paroksh Gyan</i> and <i>Prataksh Gyan</i>. <i>Paroksh Gyan</i> (also spelled <i><a href="/wiki/Paroksha" title="Paroksha">Paroksha</a>-Jnana</i>) is secondhand knowledge: knowledge obtained from books, hearsay, etc. <i>Pratyaksh Gyan</i> (also spelled <i>Pratyaksha-Jnana</i>) is the knowledge borne of direct experience, i.e., knowledge that one discovers for oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Knowledge is of special importance in the classical path of Hinduism known as <i><a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">jnana yoga</a></i> or <i>path of knowledge</i>. Its aim is to achieve oneness with the divine by fostering an understanding of the <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">self</a> and its relation to <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> or ultimate reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span></h4>
<p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_text" title="Jain text">Jain text</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tattvartha_Sutra" title="Tattvartha Sutra">Tattvartha Sutra</a>, knowledge is of five kinds:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Mati Jñāna (Sensory Knowledge)</li>
<li>Śruta Jñāna (Scriptural Knowledge)</li>
<li>Avadhi Jñāna (Clairvoyance)</li>
<li>Manah prayāya Jñāna (Telepathy)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kevala_Jnana" title="Kevala Jnana">Kevala Jnana</a> (Omniscience)</li></ul>
<p>The first two kinds of knowledge are regarded as indirect knowledge and remaining three as direct knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJain20116_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJain20116-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, knowledge (Arabic: علم, <i>ʿilm</i>) is given great significance. "The Knowing" (<i>al-ʿAlīm</i>) is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_the_Qur%27an" title="Names of God in the Qur'an">99 names</a> reflecting distinct attributes of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> asserts that knowledge comes from God (<a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D2%3Averse%3D239" rel="nofollow">2:239</a>) and various <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> encourage the acquisition of knowledge. <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is reported to have said "Seek knowledge from the cradle to the grave" and "Verily the men of knowledge are the inheritors of the prophets". Islamic scholars, theologians and jurists are often given the title <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">alim</a></i>, meaning "knowledgeble".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h4>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> tradition, knowledge (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>: דעת <i>da'ath</i>) is considered one of the most valuable traits a person can acquire. Observant Jews recite three times a day in the <a href="/wiki/Amidah" title="Amidah">Amidah</a> "You favor people with knowledge and teach mortals understanding. Favor us with your knowledge, understanding, and wisdom. Blessed are you, Adonai, who favors people with knowledge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nulman1996_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nulman1996-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> states, "A wise man is strong; yes, a man of knowledge grows in strength."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_the_knowledge_of_good_and_evil" title="Tree of the knowledge of good and evil">tree of the knowledge of good and evil</a> contained the knowledge that separated Man from God: "And the LORD God said, Behold, the man is become as one of us, to know good and evil..." (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#3:22" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 3:22</a>)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropology">Anthropology</span></h3>
<p>The <i>anthropology of knowledge</i> is a multi-disciplinary field of inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer2007_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer2007-107">[107]</a></sup> 
It studies how knowledge is acquired, stored, retrieved, and communicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2010_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2010-108">[108]</a></sup> Special interest is given to how knowledge is reproduced and undergoes changes in relation to social and cultural circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup> In this context, the term <i>knowledge</i> is used in a very broad sense, roughly equivalent to terms like <i><a href="/wiki/Understanding" title="Understanding">understanding</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth2002_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barth2002-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup> This means that the forms and reproduction of understanding are studied irrespective of their <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth value</a>. In epistemology, on the other hand, knowledge is usually restricted to forms of true belief. The main focus in <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> is on <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical observations</a> of how people ascribe truth values to meaning contents even if some of them are mistaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup> But it also includes practical components: knowledge is what is employed when interpreting and acting on the world and involves diverse phenomena, such as feelings, embodied skills, information, and concepts. It is used to understand and anticipate events in order to prepare and react accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth2002_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barth2002-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reproduction of knowledge and its changes often happen through some form of <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2010_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2010-108">[108]</a></sup> This includes face-to-face discussions and online communications as well as seminars and rituals. An important role in this context falls to <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a>, like university departments or scientific journals in the academic context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-5"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup> A <i><a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a></i> may be defined as knowledge that has been reproduced within a <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> or geographic region over several generations. However, societies also respond to various external influences, such as other societies, whose understanding is often interpreted and incorporated in a modified form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-6"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth2002_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barth2002-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important finding is that individuals belonging to the same social group usually have very similar forms of how they understand things and organize knowledge. In this regard, <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">social identities</a> play a significant role: individuals who associate themselves with similar identities, like age-influenced, professional, religious, and ethnic identities, tend to embody similar forms of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allwood2013_106-7"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2013-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Crumley-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Crumley_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCrumley2016">Crumley, Jack S.
